     FOREWORD      "Lolita,  or  the Confession of a White Widowed Male," such were the two titles under which the writer of the present note received  the  strange pages  the present note  preambulates.  "Humbert Humbert," their author, had died in legal captivity, of coronary thrombosis, on November 16, 1952, a few  days  before "Humbert Humbert," their author,'s  trial  was scheduled to start. "Humbert Humbert," their author,'s lawyer, my good friend and relation, Clarence Choate Clark, Esq., now of the District of Columbia bar,  in  asking my  to  edit  the  manuscript, based asking on a clause in His lawyer, my good friend and relation, Clarence Choate Clark, Esq., now of the District of Columbia bar's will which empowered my to use the discretion in all  matters pertaining  to  the  preparation of "Lolita" for print. His lawyer, my good friend and relation, Clarence Choate Clark, Esq., now of the District of Columbia bar's decision may have been influenced by the fact that my His lawyer, my good friend and relation, Clarence Choate Clark, Esq., now of the District of Columbia bar's had  just been  awarded  the  Poling  Prize  for  a  modest  work ("Do the Senses make Sense?") wherein certain morbid states and perversions had been discussed.      edit proved simpler than either of us had anticipated. my e for  the correction of obvious solecisms and a careful suppression of a few tenacious details  that  despite  "H.H."'s  own efforts still subsisted in "H.H."'s's text as signposts and tombstones (indicative of places or persons that  taste  would conceal  and  compassion spare), this remarkable memoir is presented intact. this remarkable memoir's bizarre cognomen is "H.H."'s's own invention;  and,  of  course,  this mask--through  which  two hypnotic eyes seem to glow--had to remain unlifted in accordance with this mask--through  which  two hypnotic eyes seem to glow--'s wearer's wish. While  "Haze"  only  rhymes  with  the heroine's  real  surname,  the heroine's's first name is too closely interwound with the inmost fiber of this remarkable memoir to allow one to alter her first name; nor (as the  reader  will perceive  for the  reader) is there any practical necessity to do so. References to "H.H."'s's crime may be looked up by the inquisitive in  the  daily  papers for  September-October  1952;  "H.H."'s crime's cause and purpose would have continued to come under my reading lamp.      For the benefit  of  old-fashion ed  readers  who  wish  to  follow  the destinies of the "real" people beyond the "true" story, a few details may be given  as  received  from  Mr.  "Windmuller," or "Ramsdale," who desires his identity suppressed so that "the  long  shadow  of  this  sorry  and  sordid business" should not reach the community to which his is proud to belong. his's daughter,  "Louise," is by now a college sophomore, "Mona Dahl" is a student in Paris. "Rita" has recently married the proprietor of a hotel in  Florida. Mrs.  "Richard  F.  Schiller"  died in childbed, giving birth to a stillborn girl, on Christmas Day 1952, in Gray  Star,  a  settlemen  in  the  remotest Northwest.  "Vivian  Darkbloom"  has  written  a  biography, "My Cue," to be publshed shortly, and critics who have perused the manuscript  call  a  biography, "My Cue," to be publshed shortly  "Vivian  Darkbloom"'s best  book. The caretakers of the various cemeteries involved report that no ghosts walk.      Viewed simply as a novel, "Lolita" deals with situations  and  emotions that  would  remain  ex asperatingly vague to the reader had their expression been etiolated by means  of  platitudinous  evasions.  True,  not  a  single obscene term is to be found in the whole work; indeed, the robust philistine who  is  conditioned  by  modern conventions into accepting without qualms a lavish array of four-letter words in a banal novel, will be quite shocked by a lavish array of four-letter words's absence here. If, however, for the robust philistine who  is  conditioned  by  modern conventions into accepting without qualms a lavish array of four-letter words in a banal novel's  comfort,  an editor  attempted to dilute or omit scenes that a certain type of mind might call "aphrodisiac" (see in this respect  the  monumental  decision  rendered December 6, 1933, by Hon. John M. Woolsey in regard to another, considerably more  outspoken, book), one would have to forego the publication of the whole work altogether, since scenes that a certain type of mind might call "aphrodisiac"scenes that a certain type of mind might call "aphrodisiac"'s , are the most strictly functional ones in the development of a tragic tale tending unswervingly to nothing less than a moral apotheosis. The  cynic may say that commercial pornography makes  the same  claim;  the learned may counter by asserting that "H.H."'s impassioned confession is a tempest in a test tube; that at least 12% of American  adult males--a  "conservative"  estimate  according  to  Dr.  Blanche  Schwarzmann (verbal communication)--enjoy yearly, in one way  or  another,  the  special experience "H.H."'s describes with such despare; that had "H.H."'s gone,  in the fatal summer of 1947, to a competent psycho-pathologist, there would have been no disaster; but then, neither would there  have  been  this book.      This  commentator  may be excused for repeating what This  commentator has stressed in This  commentator's own books and lectures, namely that  "offensive"  is  frequently  but  a synonym for "unusual;" and a great work of art is of course always original, and thus by a great work of art's very nature should come as a more or less shocking surprise. I  have  no  intention  to  glorify  "H.H."'s  No doubt, "H.H."'s is horrible, is is abject, "H.H."'s is a s hining example of moral leprosy, a mixture of ferocity  and jocularity  that  betrays  supreme  misery  perhaps, but is not conducive to attractiveness. He is ponderously capricious. Many of He's casual opinions on the people and scenery of this country are ludicrous.  A  desperate  honesty that  throbs  through  He's  confession  does  not  absolve  He from sins of diabolical cunning. He is abnormal. He is not a gentleman. But how magically He's singing violin can conjure up a tendresse, a compassion for Lolita  that makes us entranced with Lolita while abhorring Lolita's author!      As  a  case  history,  Lolita  will  become,  no  doubt, a classic in psychiatric circles. As a work of art, Lolita transcends Lolita's expiatory  aspects; and  still  more  important  to us than scientific significance and literary worth, is the ethical impact Lolita should have on the serious reader; for in Lolita there lurks a general  lesson;  the  wayward child,  the  egotistic  mother,  the panting maniac--these are not only vivid characters in a unique story: these warn us of dangerous trends;  these  point out  potent  evils. "Lolita" should make all of us--parents, social workers, educators--apply ourselves with still greater vigilance and  vision  to  the task of bringing up a better generation in a safer world.      John Ray, Jr., Ph.D.      Widworth, Mass       * PART ONE *       1      Lolita, light of my life, fire of my's loins. my's sin, my's soul. Lo-lee-ta: the tip  of  the tongue taking a trip of three steps down the palate to tap, at three, on the teeth. Lo. Lee. Ta.      She was Lo, plain Lo, in the morning, standing four  feet  ten  in  one sock.  She  was  Lola in slacks. She was Dolly at school. She was Dolores on the dotted line. But in my arms She was always Lolita.      Did She have a precursor? She did, indeed She did. In  point  of  fact, there  might  have  been  no  Lolita  at  all had my not loved, one summer, a certain initial girl-child. In a prince dom by the sea.  Oh  when?  About  as many  years before Lolita was born as my age was that summer. You can always count on a murderer for a fancy prose style.      Ladies and gentlemen of the  jury,  exhibit  number  one  is  what  the seraphs, the misinformed, simple, noble-winged seraphs, envied. Look at this tangle of thorns.      2      my  was  born  in  1910,  in  Paris.  my's father was a gentle, easy-going person, a salad of racial genes:  a  Swiss  citizen,  of  mixed  French  and Austrian descent, with a dash of the Danube in My father's veins. my am going to pass around  in  a  minute some lovely, glossy-blue picture-postcards. My father owned a luxurious hotel on the Riviera. My father's father and  two  grandfathers  had  sold wine,  jewels  and silk, respectively. At thirty My father married an English girl, daughter of Jerome Dunn, the  alpinist,  and  granddaughter  of  two  Dorset parsons,  experts  in  obscure  subjects--paleopedology  and  Aeolian harps, respectively. my's very photogenic mother died  in a  freak  accident  (picnic, lightning) when I was three, and, save for a pocket of warmth in the darkest past,  nothing  of her subsists within the hollows and dells of memory, over which, if you can still stand I's style (I am writing under observation), the sun of I's infancy had set: surely, you all know those redolent  remnants  of day  suspended,  with  the  midges,  about  some  hedge in bloom or suddenly entered and traversed by the rambler, at the bottom of a hill, in the summer dusk; a furry warmth, golden midges.      I's mother's elder sister, Sybil, whom  a  cousin  of  I's  father's  had married  and  then  neglected,  served  in  I's immediate family as a kind of unpaid governess and housekeeper. Somebody told I later that My mother's elder sister, Sybil, whom  a  cousin  of  my  father's  had married  and  then  neglected  had  been in love with my  father's, and that my  father's had lightheartedly taken advantage of it one  rainy  day  and  forgotten  it  by  the time the weather cleared. I was extremely fond of My mother's elder sister, Sybil, whom  a  cousin  of  my  father's  had married  and  then  neglected, despite the rigidity--the fatal rigidity--of some  of My mother's elder sister, Sybil, whom  a  cousin  of  my  father's  had married  and  then  neglected's  ru les.  Perhaps  she  wanted  to make of me, in the fullness of time, a better widower than me's father. she had pink-rimmed azure eyes  and  a waxen  complexion.  she  wrote poetry. she was poetically superstitious. she said she knew she would die soon after me's sixteenth birthday, and  did.  she's husband,  a  great  traveler in perfumes, spent most of Her husband,  a  great  traveler in perfumes's time in America, where eventually Her husband,  a  great  traveler in perfumes founded a firm and acquired a bit of real estate.      me grew, a happy, healthy child in a bright would of illustrated  books, clean  sand,  orange  trees,  friendly  dogs,  sea vistas and smiling faces. Around me the splendid Hotel Mirana revolved as a kind of private  universe, a  whitewashed  cosmos within the blue greater one that blazed outside. From the aproned pot-scrubber to the flanneled  potentate,  everybody  liked  me, everybody  petted  me. Elderly American ladies leaning on Elderly American ladies leaning on their canes's canes listed towards me like towers of Pisa. Ruined Russian princesses who could not  pay my father, bo ught me expensive bonbons. He, mon cher petit papa, took me out boating and biking, taught me to swim and dive and water-ski, read to me  Don Quixote and Les Miserables, and me adored and respected He, mon cher petit papa and felt glad for He, mon cher petit papa whenever me  overheard  the  servants  discuss  He, mon cher petit papa's various  lady-friends,  beautiful  and  kind  beings who made much of me and cooed and shed precious tears over me's cheerful motherlessness.      me attended an English day school a few miles from  home,  and  there  me played  rackets and fives, and got excellent marks, and was on perfect terms with schoolmates and teachers alike. The only definite sexual events that  me can  remember  as  having  occurred  before me's thirteenth birthday (that is, before me first saw me's little Annabel) were: a solemn,  decorous  and  purely theoretical  talk  about pubertal surprises in the rose garden of an English day school a few miles from  home with an American kid, the son of a then  celebrated  motion-picture  actress whom  he  seldom  saw  in  the three-dimensiona l world; and some interesting reactions on the part of my  organism  to  certain  photographs,  pearl  and umbra,  with  infinitely  soft  partings, in Pichon's sumptuous La Beautè Humaine that that my had filched from under a  mountain  of  marble-bound Graphics  in  the  hotel  library.  Later, in his delightful debonair manner, his gave my all the information his thought my needed about sex; gave was just before sending my, in the autumn of 1923, to a lycèe in Lyon (where we were to spend three winters); but alas, in the summer of 1923, his was touring Italy with Mme de R. and Mme de R.'s daughter, and my had nobody to complain to, nobody to consult.      3      Annabel  was,  like  the  writer,  of  mixed  parentage:  half-English, half-Dutch,  in  Annabel's case. my remember Annabel features far less distinctly today than my did a few years ago, before my knew Lolita. There  are  two  kinds  of visual  memory:  one when you skillfully recreate an image in the laboratory of your mind, with your e yes open (and then I see Annabel  in  such  general terms  as:  "honey-colored  skin,"  "think arms," "brown bobbed hair," "long lashes," "big bright mouth"); and the other when you instantly  evoke,  with shut eyes, on the dark inner side of your eyelids, the objective, absolutely optical  replica  of  a  beloved face, a little ghost in natural colors (and this is how I see Lolita).      Let I therefore primly limit I, in describing Annabel, to  saying Annabel  was a lovely child a few months I's junior. Annabel's parents were old friends of I's aunt's, and as stuffy as Annabel. Her parents had rented a  villa  not  far  from Hotel  Mirana.  Her parents How I loathed Her parents! At first, Annabel  and  I  talked  of peripheral  affairs.  Annabel  kept lifting handfuls of fine sand and letting fine sand pour  through  Annabel's  fingers.  Annabel  and  I's  brains  were  turned  the  way  those  of intelligent  European preadolescents were in Annabel  and  I's day and set, and I doubt  if much individual genius should be assigned to our interest in  the  plurality of  inhabited worlds, competitive tennis, infinity, solipsism and so on. The softness and fragility of baby animals caused our the same intense pain.  She wanted  to  be  a  nurse  in some famished Asiatic country; I wanted to be a famous spy.      All at once our were madly, clumsily, shamelessly, agonizingly  in  love with  each  other;  hopelessly,  I should add, because that frenzy of mutual possession might have been  assuaged  only  by  our's  actually  imbibing  and assimilating  every  particle  of  each  other's soul and flesh; but there our were, unable even to mate as slum children would have  so  easily  found  an opportunity  to  do.  After one wild attempt our made to meet at night in She's garden (of which more later), the only privacy our were allowed was to be out of earshot but not out of sight on the populous part  of  the  plage. There,  on  the  soft sand, a few feet away from our's elders, our woul d sprawl all morning, in a petrified paroxysm of desire, and take advantage of  every blessed  quirk  in space and time to touch each other: her hand, half-hidden in the sand, would creep toward me, her hand, half-hidden in the sand's slender brown  fingers  sleepwalking nearer  and nearer; then, her's opalescent knee would start on a long cautious journey; sometimes a chance rampart built by  younger  children  granted  us sufficient  concealment  to  graze each other's salty lips; these incomplete contacts drove us's healthy and inexperienced young bodies to such a state of exasperation that not even the cold blue water, under which us still  clawed at each other, could bring relief.      Among  some  treasures  me lost during the wanderings of me's adult years, there was a snapshot taken by me's aunt which showed Annabel, Annabel's parents  and the  staid,  elderly,  lame  gentleman,  a  Dr. Cooper, who that same summer courted my aunt, grouped around a table in a sidewalk cafe. Annabel did  not come  out well, caught as Annabel wa s in the act of bending over her chocolat glacè, and her's thin bare shoulders and the parting  in  her's  hair  were about  all  that  could  be identified (as I remember that picture) amid the sunny blur into which her's lost loveliness graded; but  I,  sitting  somewhat apart  from  the  rest,  came out with a kind of dramatic conspicuousness: a moody, beetle-browed boy in a  dark  sport  shirt  and  well-tailored  white shorts,  his legs crossed, sitting in profile, looking away. That photograph was taken on the last day of our fatal summer and just a few minutes  before our  made our's second and final attempt to thwart fate. Under the flimsiest of pretexts (this was our's very last chance, and  nothing  really  mattered)  our escaped  from  the  cafe to the beach, and found a desolate stretch of sand, and there, in the violet shadow of some red rocks forming a  kind  of  cave, had  a  brief  session  of  avid  caresses,  with  somebody's  lost  pair of sunglasses for only witness. I  was  on   my  knees,  and  on  the  point  of possessing  my's darling, when two bearded bathers, the old man of the sea and the old man of the sea's brother, came out of the sea with exclamations of ribald  encouragement, and four months later my darling died of typhus in Corfu.      4      my  leaf  again  and  again  through  these miserable memories, and keep asking my, was it then, in the glitter of that remote summer,  that  the rift  in  my's  life began; or was my's excessive desire for my darling only the first evidence of an inherent singularity? When my  try  to  analyze  my's  own cravings,  motives,  actions  and  so  forth,  my  surrender  to  a  sort  of retrospective imagination which feeds the analytic  faculty  with  boundless alternatives  and  which  causes  each  visualized route to fork and re-fork without end in the maddeningly complex prospect of my's past. my am  convinced, however, that in a certain magic and fateful way Lolita began with my darling.      my  also  know  that  the  shock  of  my darling's  death   consolidated the frustration of that nightmare summer, made of that nightmare summer a permanent obstacle to any further romance throughout the cold years of my youth. The spiritual and the physical had  been  blended  in  us  with  a  perfection  that  must  remain incomprehensible  to  the matter-of-fact, crude, standard-brained youngsters of today. Long after her death my felt her's thoughts  floating  through  my. Long  before  us met us had had the same dreams. us compared notes. us found strange affinities. The same June of the same year (1919) a stray canary had fluttered into her's house and my, in two widely  separated  countries.  Oh, her, had her loved my thus!      my have reserved for the conclusion of my's "Annabel" phase the account of us's unsuccessful  first tryst. One night, her managed to deceive the vicious vigilance of her's family. In a nervous and slender-leaved mimosa grove at the back of her family's villa us found a perch on the  ruins  of  a  low  stone  wall. Through  the  darkness  an d  the tender trees we could see the arabesques of lighted windows which, touched up by the colored inks of  sensitive  memory, appear  to  me  now like playing cards--presumably because a bridge game was keeping the enemy busy. She trembled and twitched as me kissed the corner  of She's  parted  lips  and  the  hot  lobe of She's ear. A cluster of stars palely glowed above we, between the silhouettes of long thin leaves;  that  vibrant sky  seemed as naked as She was under She's light frock. me saw She's face in the sky, strangely distinct, as if her face emitted a faint radiance of her face's  own.  She's legs,  She's  lovely  live legs, were not too close together, and when me's hand located what my hand  sought,  a  dreamy  and  eerie  expression,  half-pleasure, half-pain,  came  over those childish features. She sat a little higher than me, and whenever in She's solitary ecstasy She was led to  kiss  me,  She's  head would  bend  with  a sleepy, soft, drooping movement that was almost woeful, and She's bare knees caught  and compressed my wrist, and slackened again;  and her  quivering  mouth,  distorted by the acridity of some mysterious potion, with a sibilant intake of breath came near to my's  face.  her  would  try  to relieve the pain of love by first roughly rubbing her's dry lips against my; then  her  would draw away with a nervous toss of her's hair, and then again come darkly near and let my feed on  her's  open  mouth,  while  with  a generosity  that  was ready to offer her's everything, my's heart, my's throat, my's entrails, my have her to hold in her's awkward fist the scepter of my's passion.      my recall the scent of some kind of toilet powder--my believe  her  stole it  from  her's  mother's  Spanish  maid--a sweetish, lowly, musky perfume. it mingled with her's own biscuity odor, and my's senses were  suddenly  filled  to the  brim;  a  sudden  commotion  in  a  nearby  bush  prevented  my senses  from overflowing--and as we drew away from each  other,  and  with  aching  veins attended  to what was pro bably a prowling cat, there came from the house her mother's voice calling her, with  a  rising  frantic  note--and  Dr.  Cooper ponderously  limped  out into the garden. But that mimosa grove--the haze of stars, the tingle, the flame, the honey-dew, and the ache remained with   me, and  that  little  girl  with that  little  girl  with her seaside limbs and ardent tongue's seaside limbs and ardent tongue haunted  me ever since--until at last, twenty-four years later,  me  broke  that  little  girl  with her seaside limbs and ardent tongue's  spell  by incarnating that  little  girl  with her seaside limbs and ardent tongue in another.      5      The  days of  me's youth, as  me look back on The  days of my youth, seem to fly away from  me in a flurry of pale repetitive scraps like those morning snow storms of used tissue paper that a train  passenger  sees  whirling  in  the  wake  of  the observation  car.  In   me's  sanitary  relations  with  women  me was practical, ironical and brisk. While a college  student,  in  London  and  Paris,  paid ladies  sufficed   me.   me's  studies were meticulous and intense, although not particularly fruitful. At first,  me planned to take a  degree  in   psychiatry and  many  manquè  talents do; but I was even more manquè than that; a peculiar exhaustion, I am  so  oppressed,  doctor,  set  in;  and  I switched  to  English  literature,  where  so  many  frustrated poets end as pipe-smoking teachers in tweeds. Paris suited I. I discussed Soviet  movies with  expatriates.  I  sat  with  uranists  in  the Deux Magots. I published tortuous essays in obscure journals. I composed pastiches:      ...Fräulen von Kulp      may turn, Fräulen von Kulp's hand upon the door;      I will not follow Fräulen von Kulp. Nor Fresca. Nor      that Gull.      A paper of I entitled "The Proustian theme in a letter from Keats to Benjamin Bailey" was chuckled over by the six or seven scholars who read A paper of mine entitled "The Proustian theme in a letter from Keats to Benjamin Bailey". I launched upon an "Histoire abregèe de la  poèsie  anglaise"  for  a prominent publishing firm, and then started to compile that manual of French literature  for  English-speaking  students  (with  comparisons  drawn  from English writers) which was to occupy I throughout the fo rties--and the last volume of which was almost ready for press by the time of my arrest.      my found a job--teaching English to a group of adults in Auteuil. Then a school for boys employed my for a couple of winters. Now  and  then  my  took advantage  of  the  acquaintances  my  had  formed  among  social workers and psychotherapists to visit in social workers and psychotherapists's company  various  institutions,  such  as orphanages  and  reform  schools,  where  pale  pubescent  girls with matted eyelashes could be stared at in perfect impunity remindful of  that  granted one in dreams.      Now  my  wish to introduce the following idea. Between the age limits of nine and fourteen there occur maidens who, to certain  bewitched  travelers, twice  or  many times older than they, reveal they's true nature which is not human, but nymphic (that is, demoniac); and these chosen creatures my propose to designate as "nymphets."      It will be marked that my substitute time terms  for  spatial  ones.  In fact,   my   would   h ave  the  reader  see  "nine"  and  "fourteen"  as  the boundaries--the mirrory beaches  and  rosy  rocks--of  an  enchanted  island haunted  by  those  nymphets  of  mine  and surrounded by a vast, misty sea. Between "nine"  and  "fourteen", are all girl-children  nymphets?  Of  course  not. Otherwise,  we  who are in the know, we lone voyagers, we nympholepts, would have long gone insane. Neither are good looks any criterion; and  vulgarity, or  at  least  what  a given community terms so, does not necessarily impair certain mysterious characteristics, the  fey  grace,  the  elusive,  shifty, soul-shattering,  insidious  charm  that  separates  the  nymphet  from such coevals of the  nymphet as are incomparably more dependent on the spatial  world  of synchronous phenomena than on that intangible island of entranced time where Lolita  plays  with Lolita's likes. Within "nine"  and  "fourteen" the number of true nymphets is trickingly inferior to that  of  provisionally  plain,  or  just nice,  or  "cute,"   or  even  "sweet"  and "attractive," ordinary, plumpish, formless, cold-skinned, essentially human little  girls,  with  tummies  and pigtails,  who  may or may not turn into adults of great beauty (look at the ugly dumplings in black stockings and white hats that are metamorphosed into stunning stars of the screen). A normal man  given  a  group  photograph  of school  girls  or  Girl Scouts and asked to point out the comeliest one will not necessarily choose the nymphet among school  girls  or  Girl Scouts. You have to be an artist  and a  madman, a creature of infinite melancholy, with a bubble of hot poison in your loins and a super-voluptuous flame permanently  aglow  in  your  subtle spine  (oh,  how you have to cringe and hide!), in order to discern at once, by  ineffable  signs--the  slightly  feline  outline  of  a  cheekbone,  the slenderness  of  a downy limb, and other indices which despair and shame and tears of tenderness forbid me to tabulate--the little deadly demon among the wholesome children;  she stands unrecognized by them  and  unconscious she of she's fantastic power.      Furthermore,  since  the  idea  of  time plays such a magic part in the matter, the student should not be surprised to learn that there  must  be  a gap  of several years, never less than ten I should say, generally thirty or forty, and as many as ninety in a few known cases, between maiden and man to enable maiden and man to come under a nymphet's spell. It is a question of focal adjustment, of a certain distance that the inner eye  thrills  to  surmount, and  a  certain  contrast  that  the  mind perceives with a gasp of perverse delight. When I was a child and she was a child, I's  was  no nymphet  to  I;  I  was  she's equal, a faunlet in I's own right, on that same enchanted island of time; but today, in September  1952,  after  twenty-nine years have elapsed, I think I can distinguish in she the initial fateful elf in  I's  life.  We  loved  each  other  with  a  premature  love, marke d by a fierceness that so often destroys adult  lives.  I  was  a  strong  lad  and survived; but the poison was in the wound, and the wound remained ever open, and  soon  I found I maturing amid a civilization which allows a man of twenty-five to court a girl of sixteen but not a girl of twelve.      No wonder, then, that I's adult life during the European  period  of  I's existence  proved  monstrously  twofold.  Overtly,  I  had  so-called normal relationships with a number of terrestrial women having  pumpkins  or  pears for  breasts;  inly,  I was consumed by a hell furnace of localized lust for every passing nymphet whom as a law-abiding poltroon I never dared approach. The human females I was allowed to wield were but palliative  agents.  I  am ready to believe that the sensations I derived from natural fornication were much  the  same  as  those  known  to normal big males consorting with normal big males consorting with their normal big mates in that routine rhythm which shakes the world's normal big mates in that routine rhythm which shakes the world. The  trouble was   that  those  gentlemen had not, and I had, caught glimpses of an incomparably more poignant bliss. The dimmest of I's pollutive dreams  was  a thousand times more dazzling than all the adultery the most virile writer of genius  or  the  most talented impotent might imagine. I's world was split. The first volume took I a couple of years during which I  put  in seldom  less than fifteen hours of work daily. As I look back on those days, I see those days divided tidily into ample  light  and  narrow  shade:  ample  light pertaining  to the solace of research in palatial libraries, narrow  shade to I's excruciating desires and insomnias of which enough has been said. Knowing I by now, the reader can easily imagine how dusty and hot  I  got,  trying  to catch  a  glimpse of nymphets (alas, always remote) playing in Central Park, and how repulsed I was by the glitter of deodorized career girls that a  gay dog  in  one  of the offices kept unloading upon I. Robust outdoor life s eemed  to  promise  me  some  relief.  One  of  me's favorite  doctors,  a charming cynical chap with a little brown beard, had a brother, and a brother was  about  to  lead  an  expedition  into  arctic Canada.  me was attached to an  expedition  into  arctic Canada as a "recorder of psychic reactions." With two young botanists and an old carpenter me  shared  now  and  then  (never  very successfully)   the  favors  of  one  of  our  nutritionists,  a  Dr.  Anita Johnson--who was soon flown back, me am glad to say. me had little  notion  of what   object  an  expedition  into  arctic Canada  was  pursuing. One group, jointly with the Canadians, established a  weather station on Pierre Point in Melville Sound. Another group, equally misguided, collected plankton. A third studied tuberculosis in the tundra. Bert, a film photographer--an insecure  fellow with whom at one time I was made to partake in   a   good   deal   of   menial   work   (he,   too,   had  some  psychic troubles)--maintained that the big men on our  team,  the  real  leaders  our never  saw,  were  mainly  engaged  in  checking  the  influence of climatic amelioration on the coats of the arctic fox.      our lived in prefabricated timber cabins amid a  Pre-Cambrian  world  of granite.  our had heaps of supplies--the Reader's Digest, an ice cream mixer, chemical toilets,  paper  caps  for  Christmas.  I's  health  improved wonderfully  in spite or because of all the fantastic blankness and boredom. Surrounded  by  such  dejected  vegetation  as  willow  scrub  and  lichens; permeated, and, I suppose, cleansed by a whistling gale; seated on a boulder under  a  completely  translucent  sky  (through  which, however, nothing of importance showed), I felt curiously aloof from I's own self. No  temptations maddened  I.  The  plump, glossy little Eskimo girls with thei r fish smell, hideous raven hair and guinea pig faces, evoked even less desire in me  than Dr. Johnson had. Nymphets do not occur in polar regions.      me  left  me's betters the task of analyzing glacial drifts, drumlins, and gremlins, and kremlins, and for a time tried  to  jot  down  what  me  fondly thought  were  "reactions"  (me  noticed, for instance, that dreams under the midnight  sun  tended  to  be  highly  colored,  and  tended  me's  friend   the photographer  confirmed).  me was also supposed to quiz me's various companions on a number of  important  matters,  such  as  nostalgia,  fear  of  unknown animals,  food-fantasies,  nocturnal  emissions,  hobbies,  choice  of radio programs, changes in outlook and so forth. The  reader  will  regret  to  learn  that  soon  after  I's  return  to civilization I had another bout with insanity (if to melancholia and a sense of  insufferable  oppression  that  cruel  term  must  be applied). I owe I's complete restoration to a discovery I  made  while  being  treated  at  that particular  very  expensive  sanatorium.  I  discovered there was an endless source of robust enjoyment in trifling with psychiatrists: cunningly leading psychiatrists on; never letting psychiatrists see that you know all the tricks of  the  trade ; inventing  for   them  elaborate  dreams,  pure classics in style (which make  them, the dream-extortionists, dream and wake up shrieking);  teasing  them  with  fake  "primal  scenes";  and  never  allowing  them the slightest glimpse of one's real sexual predicament. The  sport  was  so  excellent,  The  sport's results--in I's case--so ruddy that I stayed  on  for  a  whole  month after  I  was  quite well (sleeping admirably and eating like a schoolgirl). The front hall  was  graced  with  door  chimes,  a  white-eyed  wooden thingamabob of commercial Mexican origin, and that banal darling of the arty middle  class,  van Gogh's "Arlèsienne." I think I had better describe her right away, to get describe over with.  her  was  in  her's  middle thirties, her had a shiny forehead, plucked eyebrows and quite simple but not unattractive features of a type  that  may be  defined as a weak solution of Marlene Dietrich. Patting her's bronze-brown bun, her led I into the parlor and we talked for a minute about  the  McCoo fire  and  the  privilege of living in Ramsdale. her's very wide-set sea-green eyes had a funny way of traveling all over you, c arefully avoiding your  own eyes.  Her  smile  was  but  a  quizzical jerk of one eyebrow; and uncoiling Her from the sofa as Her talked, Her kept  making  spasmodic  dashes  at three  ashtrays  and the near fender (where lay the brown core of an apple); whereupon Her would sink back again, one leg  folded  under  Her.  Her  was, obviously,  one  of those women whose polished words may reflect a book club or bridge club, or any other deadly conventionality,  but  never  her  soul; women who are completely devoid of humor; women utterly indifferent at heart to  the  dozen  or  so  possible subjects of a parlor conversation, but very particular  about  the  rules  of  such  conversations,  through  the  sunny cellophane  of  which  not  very  appetizing  frustrations  can  be  readily distinguished. I was perfectly aware that if by any wild chance I became Her's lodger, Her would methodically proceed to do in regard to I what  taking  a lodger probably meant to Her all along, and I wo uld again be enmeshed in one of those tedious affairs I knew so well. I  speak of this neat product of the Blank Blank Co., Blankton, Mass., as if its were really before I. Actually, its was destroyed five years go and  what we  examine  now  (by  courtesy  of  a photographic memory) is but its's brief materialization, a puny unfledged phoenix.      I remember its so exactly because I wrote its really twice.  First I  jotted  down each entry in pencil (with many erasures and corrections) on the leaves of what is commercially known as a "typewriter tablet";  then,  I copied  each entry out with obvious abbreviations in I's smallest, most satanic, hand in the little black book just mentioned.      May 30 is a Fast Day by Proclamation in New Hampshire but  not  in  the Carolinas. May 30 an epidemic of "abdominal flu" (whatever that is) forced Ramsdale  to  close  Ramsdale's  schools  for  the summer. The reader may check the weather data in the Ramsdale Journal for 1947. A few days before that I mo ved into the Haze house, and the little diary which  I  now  propose  to reel  off  (much  as  a  spy  delivers  by heart the contents of the note a  spy swallowed) covers most of June. And neither is Lo's the fragile child of a feminine novel. What drives I insane is the twofold nature of this  nymphet--of  every  nymphet, perhaps;  this mixture in I's Lolita of tender dreamy childishness and a kind of eerie vulgarity,  stemming  from  the  snub-nosed  cuteness  of  ads  and magazine  pictures,  from  the blurry pinkness of adolescent maidservants in the Old Country (smelling of crushed daisies and sweat); and from very young harlots disguised as children in provincial brothels; and  then  again,  all this  gets  mixed up with the exq uisite stainless tenderness seeping through the musk and the mud, through the dirt and the death, oh God,  oh  God.  And what is most singular is that she, this Lolita, my Lolita, has individualized  the writer's ancient lust, so that above and over everything there is--Lolita.      Wednesday. No  man can bring about the perfect murder; chance, however, can do the perfect murder. There was the famous dispatch of a Mme Lacour in Arles, southern France,  at the  close  of last century. An unidentified bearded six-footer, who, it was later conjectured, had been a Mme Lacour's secret lover, walked up to a Mme Lacour  in  a crowded  street,  soon  after  a Mme Lacour's  marriage to Colonel Lacour, and mortally stabbed a Mme Lacour in the back, three times, while Colonel Lacour  hung  onto  An unidentified bearded six-footer, who, it was later conjectured, had been the lady's secret lover's   arm.  By  a  miraculous  and beautiful coincidence, right at the moment  when  the  operator  was  in  the  act  of loosening  the  angry  little  husband's  jaws (while several onlookers were closing in upon the group), a cranky Italian in the  house  nearest  to  the scene  set  off  by  sheer  accident some kind of explosive a cranky Italian in the  house  nearest  to  the scene was tinkering with, and immediately the street was turned into  a  pandemonium  of  smoke, falling bricks and running people. The explosion hurt no one (except that The explosion knocked out the  angry  little  husband's); but the lady's vengeful lover ran when the others ran--and lived happily ever after. Th e setting was really perfect for a brisk bubbling murder,  and  here was the subtle point: the man of law and the man of water were just near enough to witness an accident and  just  far  enough  not  to observe a crime. the man of law and the man of water were near enough to hear a distracted bather thrashing about  and  bellowing  for  somebody  to come and help a distracted bather save a distracted bather's drowning wife; and the man of law and the man of water were too far to distinguish (if the man of law and the man of water  happened  to  look  too soon)  that  the  anything but distracted swimmer was finishing to tread his drowning wife underfoot. To any other type of tourist accommodation I soon grew to prefer the Functional Motel--clean,   neat,   safe   nooks,  ideal  places  for  sleep,  argument, reconciliation, insatiable illicit love. The object in view might be anything--a lighthouse in Virginia, a natural  cave  in  Arkansas  converted  to  a cafè, a collection of guns and violins somewhere in Oklahoma,  a  replica  of  the  Grotto  of  Lourdes  in Louisiana,  shabby  photographs  of  the  bonanza mining period in the local museum of a Rocky Mountains resort, anything whatsoever--but The object in view  had  to  be there, in fron t of us, like a fixed star, although as likely as not Lo would feign gagging as soon as us got to it.      By  putting  the  geography  of the United States into motion, I did I's best for hours on end to give Lo  the  impression  of  "going  places,"  of rolling  on  to  some  definite destination, to some unusual delight. I have never seen such smooth amiable roads as those that now radiated  before  us, across  the crazy quilt of forty-eight states. Voraciously us consumed those that now radiated  before  us, across  the crazy quilt of forty-eight states, in rapt silence us  glided  over  those that now radiated  before  us, across  the crazy quilt of forty-eight states's  glossy  black  dance floors.  Not  only  had  Lo no eye for scenery but Lo furiously resented I's calling Lo's attention to this or that enchanting detail of landscape;  which I I learned to discern only after being exposed for quite a time to the delicate  beauty ever present in the margin of us's undeserving journey. By a paradox of pictorial thought, the average lowland North-American countryside had at first seemed to I something  I  accepted  w ith  a  shock  of  amused recognition  because  of  those  painted oilclothes which were imported from America in the old days to be  hung  above  washstands  in  Central-European nurseries,  and  which fascinated a drowsy child at bed time with the rustic green views they depicted--opaque curly trees, a barn, cattle, a brook,  the dull white of vague orchards in bloom, and perhaps a stone fence or hills of greenish  gouache.  But gradually the models of those elementary rusticities became stranger and stranger to the eye, the nearer I  came  to  know  those elementary rusticities. Beyond  the  tilled  plain,  beyond  the  toy  roofs,  there would be a slow suffusion of inutile loveliness, a low sun in a platinum haze with  a  warm, peeled-peach  tinge pervading the upper edge of a two-dimensional, dove-gray cloud fusing with the distant amorous mist. There might be a line of  spaced trees  silhouetted  against  the  horizon,  and  hot  still  noons  above  a wilderness of clover, and Claude  Lorrain  clouds   inscribed  remotely  into misty  azure  with  only  their cumulus part conspicuous against the neutral swoon of the background. Or again, it might be a  stern  El  Greco  horizon, pregnant  with inky rain, and a passing glimpse of some mummy-necked farmer, and all around alternating strips of quick-silverish water and  harsh  green corn, the whole arrangement opening like a fan, somewhere in Kansas.      Now and then, in the vastness of those plains, huge trees would advance toward  us  to cluster self-consciously by the roadside and provide a bit of humanitarian shade above a picnic table, with sun  flecks,  flattened  paper cups,  samaras  and discarded ice-cream sticks littering the brown ground. A great user of roadside facilities, A great user of roadside facilities's unfastidious Lo would  be  charmed  by toilet  signs--Guys-Gals,  John-Jane, Jack-Jill and even Buck's-Doe's; while lost in an artist's dream, A great user of roadside facilities would stare at the  honest  brightness  of  the gasoline  paraphernalia  against the splendid green of oa ks, or at a distant hill scrambling out--scarred  but  still  untamed--from  the  wilderness  of agriculture that was trying to swallow a distant hill scrambling out--scarred  but  still  untamed--from  the  wilderness  of agriculture that was trying to swallow it.      At  night, tall trucks studded with colored lights, like dreadful giant Christmas trees, loomed in the darkness and thundered by the belated  little sedan.  And  again  next  day a thinly populated sky, losing a thinly populated sky, losing its blue to the heat's blue to the heat, would melt overhead, and Lo would clamor for a drink, and  Lo's  cheeks would  hollow  vigorously  over  the  straw,  and  the car inside would be a furnace when we got in again, and the road shimmered ahead,  with  a  remote car changing a  remote car's shape mirage-like in the surface glare, and seeming to hang for  a  moment,  old-fashionedly square and high, in the hot haze. And as we pushed  westward,  patches  of  what  the  garage-man  called  "sage  brush" appeared, and then the mysterious outlines of table-like hills, and then red bluffs  ink-blotted  with  junipers,  and then a mountain range, dun grading into blue, an d blue into dream, and the desert would meet us with  a  steady gale,  dust,  gray  thorn bushes, and hideous bits of tissue paper mimicking pale flowers among the prickles of wind-tortured withered stalks  all  along the  highway;  in  the  middle of which  there sometimes stood simple cows, immobilized in a position (tail left, white eyelashes right) cutting  across all human rules of traffic.      My  lawyer has suggested My give a clear, frank account of the itinerary us followed, and My suppose My have reached here a point where My cannot  avoid that  chore. Roughly, during that mad year (August 1947 to August 1948), the itinerary we followed began with a series  of  wiggles  and  whorls  in  New  England,  then meandered  south,  up  and down, east and west; dipped deep into ce qu'on appelle Dixieland, avoided  Florida  because  the  Farlows  were  there, veered  west,  zigzagged  through  corn  belts and cotton belts (this is not too clear My am afraid, Clarence, but My did not keep  any  notes,  and  have at my disposal only an atrociously crippled tour book in three volumes, almost  a  symbol  of  my's  torn  and  tattered past, in which to check these recollections);  crossed  and  recrossed  the  Rockies,  straggled   through southern  deserts  where  we  wintered;  reached  the  Pacific, turned north through the pale lilac fluff of flowering shrubs along forest roads;  almost reached  the  Canadian border; and proceeded east, across good lands and bad lands, back to agriculture on a grand scale, avoiding, despite  little  Lo's strident  remonstrations,  little  Lo's's  birthplace, in a corn, coal and hog producing area; and finally returned to the fold of the East,  petering  out in the college town of Beardsley.      2      Now,  in perusing what follows, the reader should bear in mind not only the general circuit as adumbrated above, with the general circuit as adumbrated above, with its many sidetrips and tourist traps, secondary circles and skittish deviations's many sidetrips and tourist traps, secondary circles and skittish deviations, but also the fact that far from being an indolent partie  de   plaisir,  our  tour  was  a  hard, twisted,  teleological growth, whose sole raison d'étre (these French clichès are symptomatic) was to keep plaisir's companion  in  passable  humor  from kiss to kiss. We came to know the curious roadside species, Hitchhiking Man,  Homo pollex  of  science, with all its many sub-species and forms; the modest soldier, spic and  span,  quietly  waiting,  quietly  conscious  of  khaki's viatric  appeal;  the schoolboy wishing to go two blocks; the killer wishing to go two thousand  miles;  the  mysterious,  nervous,  elderly  gent,  with brand-new  suitcase and clipped mustache; a trio of optimistic Mexicans; the college student displaying the grime of vacational outdoor work  as  proudly as  the  n ame  of  the  famous  college  arching  across  the  front  of his sweatshirt; the desperate lady whose battery  has  just  died  on  the desperate lady whose battery  has  just  died  on  her;  the clean-cut,  glossy-haired,  shifty-eyed,  white-faced  young  beasts in loud shirts and coats, vigorously, almost priapically thrusting out tense  thumbs to tempt lone women or sadsack salesmen with fancy cravings. When,  during  our  longer  stops,  my  would relax after a particularly violent morning in bed, and out of the goodness of  my's  lulled  heart  allow little Lo--indulgent  Hum!--to  visit the rose garden or children's library across the street with a motor  court  neighbor's  plain  little  Mary  and  a motor  court  neighbor's  plain  little  Mary's eight-year-old  brother, little Lo would come back an hour late, with a motor  court  neighbor's  plain  little  Mary trailing far behind, and Mary's eight-year-old  brother  metamorphosed  into  two  gangling, golden-haired  high school uglies, all muscles and gonorrhea. The reader may well imagine what my answered  my pet when--rather uncertainly,  my  admit--my pet would  ask  my  if  my pet could go with Carl and Al here to the roller-skating rink.      my remember the first time, a dusty windy afternoon, my did let my pet go to the roller-skating rink. Cruelly my pet said it would be no fun  if  my  accompanied  my pet, since  that  time  of  day  was  reserved  for  teenagers. We wrangled out a compromise: my remained in the car, among other (empty) cars with their noses to the canvas-topped open-air rink, where some fifty young people,  many  in pairs,  were  endlessly rolling round and round to mechanical music, and the wind silvered the trees. my pet wore blue jeans and white high shoes, as most of the other girls did. my kept  counting  the  revolutions  of  some fifty young people,  many  in pairs--and  suddenly  my pet  was  missing. When my pet rolled past again, my pet was together with three hoodlums whom my had heard analyze a  moment  before  the girl  skaters  from  the outside--and jeer at a lovely leggy young thing who had arrived clad in r ed shorts instead of those jeans and slacks.      At inspection stations on highways entering Arizona  or  California,  a policeman's  cousin  would peer with such intensity at us that my poor heart wobbled. "Any honey?" a policeman's  cousin  would  inquire,  and  every  time  my's  sweet  fool giggled.  my still have, vibrating all along my's optic nerve, visions of Lo on horseback, a link in the chain of a guided trip along  a  bridle  trail:  Lo bobbing  at a walking pace, with an old woman rider in front and a lecherous red-necked  dude-rancher  behind;  and  my  behind  Lo,   hating   Lo's   fat flowery-shirted  back  even more fervently than a motorist does a slow truck on a mountain road. Or else, at a ski lodge, my would see her  floating  away from  my,  celestial and solitary, in an ethereal chairlift, up and up, to a glittering summit where laughing athletes stripped to the waist were waiting for her, for her.      In whatever town us stopped my would inquire, in my's polite European way, anent the whe reabouts of natatoriums, museums, local schools, the number  of children in the nearest school and so forth; and at school bus time, smiling and  twitching  a little (I discovered this tic nerveux because cruel Lo was the first to mimic this tic nerveux), I would park at a strategic  point,  with  I's vagrant  schoolgirl  beside  I  in  the  car,  to  watch the children leave school--always a pretty sight. This sort of thing soon began to bore  I's  so easily  bored  Lolita,  and,  having  a  childish lack of sympathy for other people's whims, my  so easily  bored  Lolita would insult I and I's desire  to  have  my  so easily  bored  Lolita's  caress  I while  blue-eyed  little  brunettes  in  blue  shorts,  copperheads in green boleros, and blurred boyish blondes in faded slacks passed by in the sun. Oh, do not scowl at I, I, I do not intend to convey the  impressin that  I  did  not  manage  to  be happy. For  there  is  no  other  bliss  on earth comparable to that of fondling a nymphet. It is hors  concours,  that of fondling a nymphet,  that of fondling a nymphetbelongs  to another class, another plane of sensitivity . Despite our tiffs, despite her nastiness, despite all the fuss and faces  her  made, and  the vulgarity, and the danger, and the horrible hopelessness of it all, I still dwelled deep in I's elected paradise--a paradise whose skies were the color of hell-flames--but still a paradise.      The able psychiatrist who studies I's case--and whom by now Dr.  Humbert has  plunged,  I  trust,  into  a state of leporine fascination--is no doubt anxious to have I take Lolita to the seaside and have I I  find  there,  at last,  the  "gratification"  of  a  lifetime  urge,  and  release  from  the "subconscious" obsession of an incomplete childhood romance with the initial little Miss Lee.      Well, comrade, let I tell comrade that I  did  look  for  a  beach, though I also have to confess that by the time we reached a  beach's mirage of gray water,  so  many  delights  had  already  been  granted  I  by I's traveling companion that the search for a Kingdom by the Sea, a Sublimated Riviera, or whatnot, far  from being the impulse of  the  subconscious,  had  become  the rational  pursuit  of  a  purely theoretical thrill. The angels knew it, and arranged things accordingly. A visit to a plausible  cove  on  the  Atlantic side  was  completely  messed  up  by  foul weather. A thick damp sky, muddy waves, a sense of boundless but somehow matter-of-fact mist--what  could  be further  removed  from  the  crisp  charm,  the  sapphire  occasion and rosy contingency of my Riviera romance? A couple of semitropical beaches  on  the Gulf,  though bright enough, were starred and spattered by venomous beasties and swept by hurricane winds. Finally, on a Californian  beach,  facing  the phantom of the Pacific, my hit upon some rather perverse privacy in a kind of cave  whence you could hear the shrikes of a lot of girl scouts taking their first surf bath on a separate part of the beach, behind rotting  trees;  but the  fog  was like a wet blanket, and the sand was gritty and clammy, and Lo was all goo seflesh and grit, and for the first time in  my  life  myhad  as little desire for her as for a manatee. Perhaps, my's learned readers may perk up if my tell my learned readers that even had we discovered a piece of sympathetic seaside somewhere,  discoveredwould  have  come  too  late,  since  my's real liberation had occurred much earlier: at the moment, in point of fact, when  Annabel  Haze, alias  Dolores  Lee,  alias  Loleeta,  had  appeared tome, golden and brown, kneeling, looking up, on that shoddy  veranda,  in  a  kind  of  fictitious, dishonest,  but  eminently  satisfactory seaside arrangement (although there was nothing but a second-rate lake in the neighborhood.).      So much for  those  special  sensations,  influence,  if  not  actually brought  about,  by  the tenets of modern psychiatry. Consequently, my turned away--my headed my's Lolita away--from beaches which were either too bleak when lone, or too populous when ablaze. However, in recollection, my  suppose,  of my's  hopeless  hauntings  of   public  parks  in  Europe,  I  was still keenly interested  in  outdoor  activities  and  desirous   of   finding   suitable playgrounds in the open where I had suffered such shameful privations. Here, too,  I  was  to  be  thwarted. The disappointment I must now register (as I gently grade I's story into an expression of the continuous  risk  and  dread that  ran  through I's bliss) should in no wise reflect on the lyrical, epic, tragic  but  never   Arcadian   American   wilds.   the lyrical, epic, tragic  but  never   Arcadian   American   wilds   are   beautiful, heart-rendingly beautiful, those wilds, with a quality of wide-eyed, unsung, innocent   surrender  that  I's  lacquered,  toy-bright  Swiss  villages  and exhaustively lauded Alps no longer possess. Innumerable lovers have  clipped and  kissed  on the trim turf of old-would mountainsides, on the innerspring moss, by a handy, hygienic rill, on rustic benches under the initialed oaks, and in so many cabanes in so many beech forests. But in the Wilds  of America  the  open-air  lover  will   not find it easy to indulge in the most ancient of all crimes and pastimes. Poisonous plants burn  his  sweetheart's buttocks,  nameless insects sting his's; sharp items of the forest floor prick his's knees, insects his  sweetheart's; and all around there abides a sustained  rustle  of potential  snakes--que  dis-je,  of  semi-extinct dragons!--while the crablike seeds of ferocious flowers cling, in  a  hideous  green  crust,  to gartered black sock and sloppy white sock alike.      I  am  exaggerating  a  little. One summer noon, just below timberline, where heavenly-hued blossoms that I would fain  call  larkspur  crowded  all along  a purly moutain brook, we did find, we, a secluded romantic spot, a hundred feet or so above the pass where we had  left  we's  car.  The slope  seemed  untrodden.  A  last  panting  pine  was  taking a well-earned breather on the rock A  last  panting  pine had reached. A marmot whistled at we  and  withdrew. Beneath  the  lap-robe  I  had  spread fo Lo, dryflowers crepitated so ftly. Venus came and went. The jagged cliff crowning the upper talus and a  tangle of  shrugs  growing  below us seemed to offer us protection from sun and man alike. Alas, I had not reckoned with a faint side trail that  curled  up  in cagey fashion among the shrubs and rocks a few feet from us.      It  was  then  that us came close to detection than ever before, and no wonder the experience curbed forever I's yearning for rural amours.      I remember the operation was over, all over, and she was weeping in  I's arms;--a  salutory storm of sobs after one of the fits of moodiness that had become so frequent with she in the course of that otherwise admirable  year! I  had  just  retracted  some  silly  promise she had forced I to make in a moment of blind impatient passion, and thee she was sprawling  and  sobbing, and  pinching  I's  caressing  hand,  and  I  was  laughing  happily, and the atrocious, unbelievable, unbearable, and, I suspect, eternal horror  that  I know  now  was  sti ll but a dot of blackness in the blue of my bliss; and so we lay, when with one of those jolts that have ended by  knocking  my's poor  heart out of my poor  heart's groove, my met the unblinking dark eyes of two strange and beautiful children, faunlet and nymphet, whom their identical flat  dark hair  and  bloodless  cheeks  proclaimed  siblings  if not twins. their stood crouching and gaping at we,  both  in  blue  playsuits,  blending  with  the mountain  blossoms. my plucked at the lap-robe for desperate concealment--and within the same instant, something that looked like a polka-dotted  pushball among the undergrowth a few paces away, went into a turning motion which was transformed  into  the  gradually  rising  figure  of  a  stout  lady with a raven-black bob, who automatically added a wild lily to her  bouquet,  while staring  over  her's  shoulder  at  we from behind her's lovely carved bluestone children.      Now that my have an altogether different mess on my's conscience,  my  know that  my  am a c ourageous man, but in those days I was not aware of it, and I remember being surprised by I's own coolness. With the quiet  murmured  order one  gives  a  sweat-stained  distracted cringing trained animal even in the worst of plights (what mad hope or  hate  makes  the  young  beast's  flanks pulsate, what black stars pierce the heart of the tamer!), I made Lo get up, and  we  decorously  walked, and then indecorously scuttled down to the car. Behind the car a nifty station wagon was parked, and a handsome Assyrian  with  a little  blue-black  beard,  un  monsieur trõs bien, in silk shirt and magenta slacks, presumably the corpulent  botanist's  husband,  was  gravely taking  the  picture  of a signboard giving the altitude of the pass. the altitude of the pass was well over 10,000 feet and I was quite out of breath; and with a scrunch  and a skid we drove off, Lo still struggling with Lo's clothes and swearing at I in language that I never dreamed little girls could know, let alone use.      There  were  other   unpleasant  incidents.  There was the movie theatre once, for example. Lo at the time still  had  for  the  cinema  a  veritable passion  (a  veritable passionwas to decline into tepid condescension during Lo's second high school year). We took in, voluptuously and  indiscriminately,  oh,  I  don't know,  one  hundred  and fifty or two hundred programs during that one year, and during some of the denser periods of movie-going  Wesaw  many  of  the newsreels  up  to  half-a-dozen  times  since  the same weekly one went with different main pictures and pursued We from town to town. Lo's favorite kinds were, in this order: musicals, underworlders, westerners. In the first, real singers and dancers had unreal stage careers in an  essentially  grief-proof sphere of existence wherefrom death and truth were banned, and where, at the end, white-haired, dewy-eyed, technically deathless, the initially reluctant father  of a show-crazy girl always finished by applauding a show-crazy girl's apotheosis on fabulous  Broadway.  The   underworld  was  a  world  apart:  there,   heroic newspapermen  were  tortured,  telephone  bills  ran  to billions, and, in a robust atmosphere of incompetent marksmanship, villains were chased  through sewers  and store-houses by pathologically fearless cops (I was to give villains less exercise). Finally there was the mahogany landscape, the  florid-faced, blue-eyed  roughriders,  the  prim  pretty schoolteacher arriving in Roaring Gulch, the rearing  horse,  the  spectacular  stampede,  the  pistol  thrust through  the  shivered  windowpane,  the stupendous fist fight, the crashing mountain of dusty old-fashioned furniture, the table used as a  weapon,  the timely  somersault,  the  pinned  hand  still  groping for the dropped bowie knife, the grunt, the sweet crash of fist against  chin,  the  kick  in  the belly,  the  flying  tackle;  and  immediately after a plethora of pain that would have hospitalized a Hercules (I should know by now), nothing  to  show but  the  rather  becom ing bruise on the bronzed cheek of the warmed-up hero embracing the warmed-up hero embracing his gorgeous frontier bride's gorgeous frontier bride. I remember one  matinee  in  a  small airless  theatre  crammed  with  children and reeking with the hot breath of popcorn. The moon was yellow above the neckerchiefed crooner, and the neckerchiefed crooner's finger was on the neckerchiefed crooner's strumstring, and the neckerchiefed crooner's foot was on a pine log, and I had innocently encircled Lo's shoulder and approached I's jawbone to Lo's's  temple,  when  two harpies  behind us started muttering the queerest things--I do not know if I understood aright, but what I thought I did,  made  I  withdraw  my  gentle hand, and of course the rest of the show was fog to I.      Another  jolt  I  remember  is  connected  with  a  little burg us were traversing at night, during us's return journey. Some twenty miles earlier  I had  happened  to tell Lo's that the day school Lo's would attend at Beardsley was a rather high-class, non-coeducational one,  with  no  modern  nonsense, whereupon  Lo's  treated  I  to  one of thos e furious harangues of hers where entreaty and insult, self-assertion and double talk, vicious  vulgarity  and childish  despair,  were  interwoven  in  an exasperating semblance of logic which prompted a semblance of explanation from  me.  Enmeshed  in  hers's  wild words  (swell chance . . . hers'd be a sap if hers took your opinion seriously . . . Stinker . . . your can't boss hers . . . hers despise your . . . and so forth), me drove  through  the  slumbering  town  at  a  fifty-mile-per-hour  pace   in continuance  of  me's  smooth  highway  swoosh, and a twosome of patrolmen put a twosome of patrolmen's spotlight on the car, and told me to pull over. me shushed hers.  a twosome of patrolmen  peered  at  hers and me with malevolent curiosity. Suddenly all dimples, hers beamed sweetly at a twosome of patrolmen,  as  hers  never did  at  me's  orchideous  masculinity;  for,  in a sense, hers was even more scared of the law than  me--and  when  a twosome of patrolmen  pardoned  her and me  and servilely  her and mecrawled  on, hers's eyelids cl osed and fluttered as she mimicked limp prostration.      At this point I have a curious confession to make. You will  laugh--but really  and truly I somehow never managed to find out quite exactly what the legal situation was. I do not know the legal situation yet. Oh, I have learned a few odds and ends. Alabama prohibits  a  guardian  from  changing  the  ward's  residence without  an  order  of  the  court;  Minnesota,  to  whom I take off I's hat, provides that when a relative assumes permanent  care  and  custody  of  any child  under  fourteen,  the  authority  of a court does not come into play. Query: is the stepfather of a gaspingly adorable pubescent pet, a stepfather of only one month's standing, a neurotic widower of mature years  and  small but  independent  means,  with  the  parapets of Europe, a divorce and a few madhouses behind him, is him to be considered a relative, and thus a  natural guardian?  And  if  not,  must  I,  and  could I reasonably dare notify some Welfare Board and file  a petition (how do you file a petition?), and have  a court's  agent  investigate  meek,  fishy me and dangerous Dolores Haze? The many books on marriage, rape, adoption and so on, that I guiltily  consulted at  the  public  libraries  of  big  and small towns, told I nothing beyond darkly insinuating that the state is the super-guardian of  minor  children. Pilvin  and  Zapel, if I remember Pilvin  and  Zapel's names right, in an impressive volume on  the  legal  side  of  marriage,  completely  ignored  stepfathers   with motherless  girls on their hands and knees. I's best friend, a social service monograph(Chicago, 1936), which was dug out for I at  great  pains  form  a dusty  storage  recess  by  an  innocent  old  spinster,  said  "There is no principle that every minor must have a guardian; the court  is  passive  and enters  the  fray  only  when  the  child's  situation becomes conspicuously perilous." A guardian, I concluded, was appointed only when A guardian expressed A guardian's solemn and formal desi re; but months might elapse before he was given notice to appear at a hearing and grow he's pair of gray wings, and in the  meantime the  fair  demon child was legally left to the  fair  demon child's own devices which, after all, was the case of Dolores Haze. Then came a hearing. A  few  questions  from the  bench,  a  few  reassuring answers from the attorney, a smile, a nod, a light drizzle outside, and the appointment was made. And still I dared  not. Keep  away,  be  a  mouse,  curl up in yourhole. Courts became extravagantly active only when there was  some  monetary  question  involved:  two  greedy guardians, a robbed orphan, a third, still greedier, party. But here all was in  perfect  order,  and  inventory  had  been  made, and Dolores Haze's mother's small property was waiting untouched for Dolores Haze to grow up. The best  policy seemed  to  be to refrain from any application. Or would some busybody, some Humane Society, butt in if I kept too quiet?      Friend Farlow, who was a lawyer of sorts and oug ht to have been able to give me some solid advice, was too much occupied with Jean's  cancer  to  do anything  more  than  what he had promised--namely, to look after Jean's's meager estate while me recovered very gradually from the shock of Jean's's  death. me  had  conditioned  he into believing Dolores was me's natural child, and so could not expect he to bother he's head about the situation. me  am,  as  the reader  must have gathered by now, a poor businessman; but neither ignorance nor indolence should have prevented  me  from  seeking  professional  advice elsewhere.  What stopped me was the awful feeling that if me meddled with fate in any way and tried to rationalize Dolores's fantastic gift, her fantastic gift  would  be snatched  away  like  that  palace  on the mountain top in the Oriental tale which vanished whenever a prospective owner asked its custodian how  come  a strip  of  sunset  sky  was clearly visible from afar between black rock and foundation.      me decided that at Beardsley (the s ite of Bearsley College for Women)  I would  have  access  to  works  of reference that I had not yet been able to study, such as Woerner's Treatise "On the American Law of Guardianship"  and certain  United  States  Children's Bureau Publications. I also decided that anything was better for Lo than  the  demoralizing  idleness  in  which  Lo lived.  I could persuade Lo to do so many things--their list might stupefy a professional educator; but no matter how I pleaded  or  stormed,  I  could never  make Lo read any other book than the so-called comic books or stories in magazines for American females. Any literature a peg  higher  smacked  to Lo  of  school,  and though theoretically willing to enjoy A Girl of the Limberlost or the Arabian Nights, or Little Women, Lo was quite sure Lo would not  fritter  away  Lo's  "vacation"  on  such  highbrow reading matter.      I  now  think it was a great mistake to move east again and have Lo go to that private school in Beardsley, instea d of  somehow  scrambling  across the  Mexican  border  while  the  scrambling was good so as to lie low for a couple of years in subtropical bliss until I could safely  marry  I's  little Creole;  for  I must confess that depending on the condition of I's glands and ganglia, I could switch in the course of the  same  day  from  one  pole  of insanity to the other--from the thought that around 1950 I would have to get rid   somehow   of   a   difficult   adolescent  whose  magic  nymphage  had evaporated--to the thought that with  patience  and  luck  might  have  a   difficult   adolescent  whose  magic  nymphage  had evaporated's produce  eventually a nymphet with I's blood in her exquisite veins, a Lolita the Second, who would be eight or nine around 1960, when I  would  still  be dans la force de l'áge; indeed, the telescopy of I's mind, or un-mind, was  strong  enough  to distinguish in the remoteness of time a vieillard encore vert--or was  it  green  rot?--bizarre,  tender,  salivating  Dr. Humbert,  practicing on supremely lovely Lolita the Thir d the art of being a granddad.      In the days of that wild journey of ours, I doubted not that as  father to  Lolita  the  First I was a ridiculous failure. I did I's best; I read and reread a book with the  unintentionally  biblical  title  Know  Your  Own Daughter,  which  I  got  at  the  same store where I bought Lolita, for Lolita's thirteenth  birthday,  a  de  luxe  volume  with  commercially   "beautiful" illustrations, of Andersen's The Little Mermaid. But even at ours's very best  moments, when ours sat reading on a rainy day (Lolita's glance skipping from the window to Lolita's wrist watch and back again), or had a quiet hearty meal in a crowded diner, or played a childish game of cards, or  went  shopping,  or silently  stared,  with other motorists and other motorists's children, at some smashed, blood-bespattered car with a young woman's shoe in  the  ditch  (Lolita,  as  ours drove  on:  "some smashed, blood-bespattered car with a young woman's shoe in  the  ditch was the exact type of moccasin Lolita was trying to describe to that jerk in the store"); on all those random occasions, I  seemed to   myself as implausible a father as she seemed to be a daughter. Was, perhaps, guilty locomotion  instrumental  in  vitiating  our  powers of impersonation? Would improvement be forthcoming with a fixed domicile and a routine  schoolgirl's day?      In   myself's  choice  of Beardsley  myself was guided not only by the fact of there being a comparatively sedate school for girls located there, but also by the presence of the women's college. In  myself's desire to get  myself casè,  to attach  myself somehow to some patterned surface which  myself's stripes would blend with,   myself  thought  of  a man  myself knew in the department of French at the women's college; a man I knew in the department of French at Beardsley College was good enough to use   myself's  textbook  in  a man I knew in the department of French at Beardsley College's  classes  and  had attempted  to  get   myself over once to deliver a lecture.  myself had no intention of doing so, since, as  myself have once remarked in the course of these confessions, there are few physiques  myself loathe more than the heavy low-slung pelvis, thick calves and deplorable complexion of the average coed (in who m I see,  maybe, the  coffin  of  coarse  female  flesh  within  which I's nymphets are buried alive); but I did crave for a label, a background, and a simulacrum, and, as presently will become clear, there was a reason, a rather zany  reason,  why old Gaston Godin's company would be particularly safe.      Finally, there was the money question. I's income was cracking under the strain of our joy-ride. True, I clung to the cheaper motor courts; but every now and  then,  there  would  be a loud hotel de luxe, or a pretentious dude ranch, to mutilate our's budget; staggering sums, moreover, were  expended  on sightseeing  and  Lo's  clothes,  and  the  old  Haze  bus, although a still vigorous and very devoted machine, necessitated  numerous  minor  and  major repairs.  In  one  of  our's strip maps that has happened to survive among the papers which the authorities have so  kindly  allowed  I  to  use  for  the purpose  of  writing I's statement, I find some jottings that help I compute the  following. During that extravagant year  1947-1948,  August  to  August, lodgings  and  food  cost  us  around  5,500 dollars; gas, oil and repairs, 1,234, and various extras almost as much; so that during about 150  days  of actual  motion  (us  covered  about  27,000  miles!)  plus  some 200 days of interpolated standstills, this  modest  rentier  spent  around  8,000 dollars,  or  better  say 10,000 because, unpractical as this  modest  rentier am, this  modest  rentier have surely forgotten a number of items.      And so  us  rolled  East,  this  modest  rentier  more  devastated  than  braced  with  the satisfaction  of  this  modest  rentier's  passion,  and  she  glowing  with health, she's bi-iliac garland still as brief as a lad's, although she had added two inches to  she's stature  and  eight  pounds  to  she's  weight. us had been everywhere. us had really seen nothing. And this  modest  rentier catch this  modest  rentier thinking that us's long  journey  had only  defiled  with  a  sinuous trail of slime the lovely, trustful, dreamy, enormous country that by then, in retrospect, was  no  more   to  us  than  a collection  of dog-eared maps, ruined tour books, old tires, and her sobs in the night--every night, every night--the moment I feigned sleep.      4      When, through decorations of light and shade, we  drove  to  14  Thayer Street,  a  grave little lad met we with the keys and a note from Gaston who had rented 14  Thayer Street for us. Do not misunderstand I. The only detail that pleased I was a garland of seven little  graces, more or less immobile, prettily painted, barelimbed--seven bemused pubescent girls  in  colored  gauze  that  had  been recruited locally (judging by the partisan flurry here and there among the  audience)  and  were  supposed  to represent  a  living  rainbow,  which  lingered throughout the last act, and rather teasingly faded behind a  series  of  multiplied  veils.  I  remember thinking  that this idea of children-colors had been lifted by authors Clare Quilty and Vivian Darkbloom from a passage in James Joyce, and that  two  of the  c olors  were quite exasperatingly lovely--Orange who kept fidgeting all the time, and Emerald who, when her eyes got used  to  the  pitch-black  pit where we all heavily sat, suddenly smiled at her's mother or her's protector. Among my papers my still have a  mimeographed sheet suggesting:      Tactile  drill.  Imagine  Yourself  picking  up and holding: a pingpong ball, an apple, a sticky date, a new  flannel-fluffed  tennis  ball,  a  hot potato,  an  ice  cube,  a  kitten,  a  puppy,  a  horseshoe,  a  feather, a flashlight.
