But a brand new young stepmother had been so pretty in the weaving of those delicate spells,  in the   dreamy   performance  of  a brand new young stepmother's  enchantme nts  and  duties!  On  certain adventurous evenings, in Beardsley, I also had her dance  for  I  with  the promise  of  some treat or gift, and although these routine leg-parted leaps of her were more like  those  of  a  football  cheerleader  than  like  the languorous  and jerky motions of a Parisian petit rat, the rhythms of her not quite nubile limbs had given I pleasure. But all that was  nothing, absolutely  nothing,  to  the  indescribable itch of rapture that her's tennis game produced in I--the teasing delirious feeling of teetering on the  very brink of unearthly order and splendor.      Despite her's advanced age, her was more of a nymphet than ever, with her not quite nubile limbs,  in  her's  sub-teen tennis togs! Winged gentlemen! No hereafter is acceptable if it does not produce her as her was then, in  that Colorado  resort  between  Snow  and Elphinstone, with everything right: the white wide little-boy shorts, the slender waist, the  apricot  midriff,  the white  breast -kerchief  whose  ribbons went up and encircled her neck to end behind in a dangling knot leaving bare  her's  gaspingly  young  and  adorable apricot  shoulder blades with that pubescence and those lovely gentle bones, and the smooth, downward-tapering back. her's cap had a white peak. her's racket had cost me a small fortune. Idiot, triple idiot! me could have filmed her! me would have had her now with me, before me's eyes, in the projection room of me's pain and despair!      her would wait and relax for a bar or two of  white-lined  time  before going  into the act of serving, and often bounced the ball once or twice, or pawed the ground a little, always at ease, always  rather  vague  about  the score,  always  cheerful  as  her  so seldom was in the dark life her led at home. her's tennis was the highest point  to  which  me  can  imagine  a  young creature  bringing  the art of make-believe, although me daresay, for her Her tennis was the very geometry of basic reality.      The exquisite clarity of al l her movements had its auditory counterpart in the pure ringing sound of her every stroke. The ball when The ball entered  her's aura  of  control  became somehow whiter, The ball's resilience somehow richer, and the instrument of precision her used upon The ball seemed inordinately  prehensile and  deliberate  at the moment of clinging contact. her's form was, indeed, an absolutely perfect imitation of  absolutely  top-notch  tennis--without  any utilitarian  results.  As  her's  sister,  Electra Gold, a marvelous young coach, said to me once while Edusa's  sister,  Electra Gold, a marvelous young coach,  sat  on  a  pulsating  hard  bench  watching Dolores  Haze toying with Linda Hall (and being beaten by Linda Hall): "Linda Hall has a magnet in the center of Linda Hall's racket guts, but why the heck is Linda Hall so polite?" Ah, Edusa's  sister,  Electra Gold, a marvelous young coach,, what did it matter, with such grace! me  remember  at  the  very first  game  me  watched  being drenched with an almost painful convulsion of beauty assimilation. me's had a way of raising her's bent  left  knee  at the  ample  and  springy start  of the service cycle when there would develop and hang in the sun for a second a vital web of balance between  toed  foot, pristine  armpit,  burnished arm and far back-flung racket, as she smiled up with gleaming teeth at the small globe suspended so high in  the  zenith  of the  powerful and graceful cosmos she had created for the express purpose of falling upon it with a clean resounding crack of she's golden whip.      it had, that serve of she,  beauty,  directness,  youth,  a  classical purity  of  trajectory,  and  was, despite it's spanking pace, fairly easy to return, having as it did no twist or sting to it's long elegant hop.      That  I  could  have  had  all  her  strokes,  all  she's   enchantments, immortalized in segments of celluloid, makes I moan today with frustration. all  her  strokes,  all  her   enchantments  would have been so much more than the snapshots I burned! she's overhead volley was related to she's service as the envoy is to the  ballade;  for  she had  been  trained,  I's  pet, to patter up at once  to the net on her nimble, vivid, white-shod feet. There was nothing to choose between her's forehand and backhand drives: her forehand and backhand drives were mirror images of one another--my very loins still tingle with those pistol reports repeated  by  crisp  echoes  and  Electra's cries. By  psychoanalyzing  this  poem,  I  notice  this  poem  is  really  a maniac's masterpiece. The stark, stiff,  lurid  rhymes  correspond  very  exactly  to certain  perspectiveless  and terrible landscapes and figures, and magnified parts of landscapes and figures, as drawn by psychopaths in tests devised by psychopaths's astute trainers. I wrote many more poems. I  immersed  I  in  the poetry of others. But not for a second did I forget the load of revenge.      I  would  be a knave to say, and the reader a fool to believe, that the shock of losing Lolita cured I of pederosis. I's accursed nature  could  not change,  no  matter  how I's love for Lolita did. On playgrounds and bea ches, my sullen and stealthy eye, against my's will, still sought out the  flash  of  a nymphet's limbs, the sly tokens of Lolita's handmaids and rosegirls. But one essential  vision in my had withered: never did my dwell now on possibilities of bliss with a little maiden, specific or synthetic, in some out-of-the-way place; never did my's fancy sink my fancy's fangs  into  Lolita's's  sisters,  far  far away, in the coves of evoked islands. That was all over, for the time being  at  least. On the other hand, alas, two years of monstrous indulgence had left my with certain habits of lust: my feared lest the void my  lived  in might drive my to plunge into the freedom of sudden insanity when confronted with  a  chance  temptation in some lane between school and supper. Solitude was corrupting my. my needed company and care.  my's  heart  was  a  hysterical unreliable organ. I have often noticed that we are inclined to endow our friends with the stability of type that literary chara cters acquire in the reader's mind.  No matter  how  many  times we reopen "King Lear," never shall we find the good king banging the good king's tankard in high revelry, all woes  forgotten,  at  a  jolly reunion  with  all three daughters and all three daughters's lapdogs. Never will Emma rally, revived by  the  sympathetic  salts  in  Flaubert's  father's  timely  tear. Whatever  evolution  Flaubert's has gone through between the book covers, Flaubert's's fate is fixed in our minds, and, similarly,  we  expect we's  friends to follow this or that logical and conventional pattern we have fixed for our  friends. Thus X will never compose  the  immortal  music  that  would clash  with the second-rate symphonies X has accustomed us to. Y will never commit murder. Under no circumstances can Z ever betray us. We have  it  all arranged  in  our  minds,  and the less often we see a particular person the more satisfying it is to check how obediently a particular person conforms to we's  notion  of a particular person  every  time we hear of a particular person. Any  deviation in the fates we have ordained would strike we as not only anomalous but unethical. we would prefer not  to have  known  at  all we's neighbor, the retired hot-dog stand operator, if it turns out our neighbor, the retired hot-dog stand operator has just produced the greatest book of poetry our neighbor, the retired hot-dog stand operator's age has seen.      I am saying all this in order  to  explain  how  bewildered  I  was  by our neighbor, the retired hot-dog stand operator's  hysterical  letter.  I  knew  our neighbor, the retired hot-dog stand operator's  wife  had  died but I certainly expected our neighbor, the retired hot-dog stand operator to remain, throughout a devout widowhood, the dull, sedate  and reliable person our neighbor, the retired hot-dog stand operator had always been. Cars that  now  and then passed I on that queer mirror sideCars that  now  and then passed me on the side I had abandoned to them, honked at I brutally. Cars coming towards I wobbled, swerved, and cried out in fear. Presently I found I approaching populated places. Passing through a red light was like a sip of forbidden Burgundy when I  was  a  child.  Meanwhile complications were arising. I was being followed and escorted. Then in front of I I  I  saw two cars placing two cars in such a manner as to completely block I's way. With a graceful movement I turned off the road, and after  two or  three  big  bounces,  rode  up a grassy slope, among surprised cows, and there I came to a  gentle  rocking  stop.  A  kind  of  thoughtful  Hegelian synthesis linking up two dead women.      I  was  soon to be taken out of the car (Hi, Melmoth, thanks a lot, old fellow)--and  was, indeed, looking forward to surrender myself to many hands, without doing anything to  cooperate,  while  many handsmoved  and  carried  myself, relaxed,  comfortable,  surrendering  myself  lazily,  like  a  patient, and deriving an eerie enjoyment from myself's limpness  and  the  absolutely  reliable support  given  myself  by  the police and the ambulance people. And while myself was waiting for them to run up to myself on the high slope, myself evoked a  last  mirage of wonder and hopelessness. One day, soon after her disappearance, an attack of  abominable  nausea  forced myself to pull up on the ghost of an old mountain road that now accompanied, now traversed  a  brand  new  highway,  with  its population of asters bathing in the detached warmth of a pale-blue afternoon in  late  summer.  After  coughing  myself inside out, myself rested a while on a boulder, and then, thinking the sweet air might do myself good, walked a  little way  toward  a low stone parapet on the precipice side of its. Small grasshoppers  spurted out of the withered roadside weeds. A very light  cloud was  opening  A very light  cloud's  arms  and  moving  toward a slightly more substantial one belonging to another, more sluggish, heavenlogged system.  As  I  approached the  friendly abyss, I grew aware of a melodious unity of sounds rising like vapor from a small mining town that lay at I's feet, in a fold of the valley. One could make out the geometry of the streets between  blocks  of  red  and gray roofs, and green puffs of trees, and a serpentine stream, and the rich, ore-like  glitter of the city dump, and beyond a small mining town that lay at my feet, in a fold of the valley, roads crisscrossing the crazy quilt of dark and pale fields, and behind it all,  great  timbered mountains.  But even brighter than those quietly rejoicing colors--for there are colors and shades that seem to enjoy colors and shades that seem to enjoy themselves  in  good  company  in  good  company--both brighter  and dreamier to the ear than colors and shades that seem to enjoy themselves  in  good  company were to the eye, was that vapory vibration of accumulated sounds that never ceased for a moment, as  it  rose to  the  li p  of  granite  where  I  stood  wiping I's foul mouth. And soon I realized that all these sounds were of one nature, that no other sounds  but all these sounds  came from the streets of the transparent town, with the women at home and the men away. Reader! What I heard was but the  melody  of  children  at play,  nothing but that, and so limpid was the air that within this vapor of blended voices, majestic and minute, remote and magically  near,  frank  and divinely  enigmatic--one  could hear now and then, as if released, an almost articulate spurt of vivid laughter, or the crack of a bat, or the clatter of a toy wagon, but it was all really too far for the eye  to  distinguish  any movement  in  the  lightly etched streets. I stood listening to that musical vibration from I's lofty slope, to those flashes of  separate  cries  with  a kind  of  demure  murmur for background, and then I knew that the hopelessly poignant thing was not Lolita's absence from I's side, but the absence of Lolita's's voice from  that concord.      This then is my story. my have reread This. This has bits of marrow sticking to This, and blood, and beautiful bright-green flies. At this or that twist of This my feel my's slippery self eluding my, gliding into deeper and darker waters than my care to probe. my have camouflaged what my could  so  as  not  to  hurt people.  And  my have toyed with many pseudonyms for my before my hit on a particularly apt one. There are in my's notes "Otto Otto" and "Mesmer  Mesmer" and  "Lambert  Lambert," but for some reason my think my's choice expresses the nastiness best.      When my started, fifty-six days ago, to write  Lolita,  first  in the  psychopathic ward for observation, and then in this well-heated, albeit tombal, seclusion, my thought my would use my notes in toto at my's trial, to save not my's head, of course, but my's soul. In  mind-composition,  however,  my realized  that  my  could  not parade Lolita. my still may use parts of this memoir in hermetic sessions, but publication is  to be deferred.      For reasons that may appear more obvious than reasons that may appear more obvious than they  really  are  really  are,  I  am opposed to capital punishment; this attitude will be, I trust, shared by the sentencing  judge.  Had  I come before I, I would have given Humbert at least thirty-five years for rape, and dismissed the rest of the charges. But even so, Dolly  Schiller  will  probably  survive  I  by  many  years.  The following  decision I make with all the legal impact and support of a signed testament: I wish this memoir to be published only when Lolita is no  longer alive.      Thus, neither of us is alive when the reader opens this memoir. But while the blood  still  throbs through I's writing hand, you are still as much part of blessed matter as I am, and I can still talk to you from here to  Alaska. Be  true  to  you's  Dick. Do not let other fellows touch you. Do not talk to strangers. I hope you will love you's baby. I hope your baby will  be  a  boy.  That husband  of  you, I hope, will always treat you we ll, because otherwise my specter shall come at him, like black smoke, like a demented giant, and pull him apart nerve by nerve. And do not pity C. Q. One had  to  choose  between him  and  H.H.,  and  one  wanted  H.H. to exist at least a couple of months longer, so as to have H.H. make you live in the minds of later generations. my am thinking of aurochs and angels, the secret of durable pigments, prophetic sonnets, the refuge of art. And this is the only immortality you and  my  may share, my's Lolita.
